<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;left:0;top:0;border-width:0 200px 100px 0;border-color:orange"></div><div style="border:solid transparent;background-color: initial;position:absolute;width:100px;line-height:0;right:100px; top:0; border-width:0 137.63819204712px 100px 0; border-right-color:red"></div>
<div style="position:absolute;line-height:0;border-style:solid;right:0;top:0;border-width:0 100px 100px 0;border-color:red"></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<p>Global <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse gas emissions">greenhouse gas emissions</a> from transportation:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:red; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Car#Environmental_effects" title="Car">Cars</a> (40%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:orange; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Truck#Environmental_effects" title="Truck">Trucks</a> (34%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:navy; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_aviation" title="Environmental effects of aviation">Planes</a> (11%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:teal; color:white;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_effects_of_shipping" title="Environmental effects of shipping">Boats</a> (11%)</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:silver; color:black;"> </span> <a href="/wiki/Train" title="Train">Trains</a> (4%)</div>
</div>
</div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_transport" title="Environmental impact of transport">Environmental impact of transport</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG"><img alt="Looking down a busy road, which is banked on both sides by tall buildings, some of which are covered in advertisement billboards" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG/220px-Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG/330px-Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG/440px-Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Traffic_jam_Sao_Paulo_09_2006_30.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">Traffic congestion</a> persists in <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, Brazil, despite the no-drive days based on license numbers.</div></div></div>
<p>Transport is a major use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energy_(society)" title="Energy (society)">energy</a> and burns most of the world's <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a>. This creates air pollution, including <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxides</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Particulate" title="Particulate">particulates</a>, and is a significant contributor to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a> through emission of <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas_pdf_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-pnas_pdf-35">[35]</a></sup> for which transport is the fastest-growing emission sector.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> By sub-sector, road transport is the largest contributor to global warming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_regulation" title="Environmental regulation">Environmental regulations</a> in developed countries have reduced individual vehicles' emissions; however, this has been offset by increases in the numbers of vehicles and in the use of each vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pnas_pdf_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-pnas_pdf-35">[35]</a></sup> Some pathways to reduce the carbon emissions of road vehicles considerably have been studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Energy use and emissions vary largely between modes, causing <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalists</a> to call for a transition from air and road to rail and human-powered transport, as well as increased <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transport_electrification" title="Transport electrification">transport electrification</a> and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">energy efficiency</a>. 
</p><p>Other environmental impacts of transport systems include <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">traffic congestion</a> and automobile-oriented <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a>, which can consume natural habitat and agricultural lands. By reducing transport emissions globally, it is predicted that there will be significant positive effects on Earth's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_quality" title="Air quality">air quality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">smog</a>, and climate change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Electric_car" title="Electric car">electric cars</a> are being built to cut down <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CO2_emission" title="CO2 emission">CO2 emission</a> at the point of use, an approach that is becoming popular among cities worldwide is to prioritize public transport, bicycles, and <a href="/wiki/Pedestrian" title="Pedestrian">pedestrian movement</a>. Redirecting vehicle movement to create 20-minute neighbourhoods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> that promotes <a href="/wiki/Exercise" title="Exercise">exercise</a> while greatly reducing vehicle dependency and pollution. Some policies are levying a congestion charge<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> to cars for travelling within congested areas during peak time.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_development">Sustainable development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sustainable development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The United Nations first formally recognized the role of transport in <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a> in the 1992 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Earth_Summit_2012" title="United Nations Earth Summit 2012">United Nations Earth summit</a>. In the 2012 United Nations World Conference, global leaders unanimously recognized that transport and mobility are central to achieving the sustainability targets. In recent years, data has been collected to show that the transport sector contributes to a quarter of the global <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas emissions</a>, and therefore <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_transport" title="Sustainable transport">sustainable transport</a> has been mainstreamed across several of the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">2030 Sustainable Development Goals</a>, especially those related to food, security, health, energy, economic growth, infrastructure, and cities and human settlements. Meeting sustainable transport targets is said to be particularly important to achieving the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Agreement" title="Paris Agreement">Paris Agreement</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are various <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> (SDGs) that are promoting sustainable transport in order to meet the defined goals. These include <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_3" title="Sustainable Development Goal 3">SDG 3</a> on health (increased road safety), <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_7" title="Sustainable Development Goal 7">SDG 7</a> on energy, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_8" title="Sustainable Development Goal 8">SDG 8</a> on decent work and economic growth, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_9" title="Sustainable Development Goal 9">SDG 9</a> on resilient infrastructure, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_11" title="Sustainable Development Goal 11">SDG 11</a> on sustainable cities (access to transport and expanded public transport), <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_12" title="Sustainable Development Goal 12">SDG 12</a> on sustainable consumption and production (ending <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel_subsidies" title="Fossil fuel subsidies">fossil fuel subsidies</a>), and SDG 14 on oceans, seas, and marine resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_transport" title="History of transport">History of transport</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_transportation_technology" title="Timeline of transportation technology">Timeline of transportation technology</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronocice_drawn.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bronocice_drawn.png/220px-Bronocice_drawn.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bronocice_drawn.png/330px-Bronocice_drawn.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Bronocice_drawn.png/440px-Bronocice_drawn.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronocice_drawn.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Bronocice_pot" title="Bronocice pot">Bronocice pot</a> with the earliest known image of a wheeled vehicle in the world, found in <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bullock_team.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="896" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Bullock_team.jpg/220px-Bullock_team.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Bullock_team.jpg/330px-Bullock_team.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Bullock_team.jpg/440px-Bullock_team.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bullock_team.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">bullock</a> team hauling <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a> in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Natural">Natural</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Natural">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Humans' first ways to move included walking, running, and swimming. The <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of animals introduced a new way to lay the burden of transport on more powerful creatures, allowing the hauling of heavier loads, or humans riding animals for greater speed and duration. Inventions such as the wheel and the sled (U.K. sledge) helped make animal transport more efficient through the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Vehicle" title="Vehicle">vehicles</a>.
</p><p>The first forms of <a href="/wiki/Road_transport" title="Road transport">road transport</a> involved animals, such as <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a> (<a href="/wiki/Domestication_of_the_horse" title="Domestication of the horse">domesticated</a> in the 4th or the 3rd millennium BCE), <a href="/wiki/Ox" title="Ox">oxen</a> (from about 8000 BCE),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> or humans carrying goods over <a href="/wiki/Dirt" title="Dirt">dirt</a> tracks that often followed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game (food)">game</a> <a href="/wiki/Trail" title="Trail">trails</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure_2">Infrastructure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Infrastructure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many early civilizations, including those in <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley</a>, constructed paved roads.  In <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_Empire" title="Persian Empire">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> empires built stone-paved roads to allow armies to travel quickly. Deep roadbeds of crushed stone underneath kept such roads dry. The medieval <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> later built <a href="/wiki/Tarmacadam" title="Tarmacadam">tar-paved</a> roads. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_transport">Water transport</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Water transport">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Water transport, including rowed and sailed vessels, dates back to <a href="/wiki/Time_immemorial" title="Time immemorial">time immemorial</a> and was the only efficient way to transport large quantities or over large distances prior to the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>. The first watercraft were <a href="/wiki/Canoe" title="Canoe">canoes</a> cut out from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tree_trunk" title="Tree trunk">tree trunks</a>. Early water transport was accomplished with ships that were either rowed or used the <a href="/wiki/Wind" title="Wind">wind</a> for propulsion, or a combination of the two. The importance of water has led to most cities that grew up as sites for trading being located on rivers or on the sea-shore, often at the intersection of two bodies of water. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical">Mechanical</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Transport&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Mechanical">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Until the Industrial Revolution, transport remained slow and costly, and production and consumption gravitated as close to each other as feasible.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a> in the 19th century saw several inventions fundamentally change transport. With <a href="/wiki/Telegraphy" title="Telegraphy">telegraphy</a>, communication became instant and independent of the transport of physical objects. The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Steam_engine" title="Steam engine">steam engine</a>, closely followed by its application in <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">rail transport</a>, made land transport independent of human or animal muscles. Both speed and capacity increased, allowing specialization through manufacturing being located independently of natural resources. The 19th century also saw the development of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steam_ship" title="Steam ship">steam ship</a>, which sped up global transport.
</p><p>With the development of the <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">combustion engine</a> and the automobile around 1900, road transport became more competitive again, and mechanical private transport originated. The first "modern" highways were constructed during the 19th century<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> with <a href="/wiki/Macadam" title="Macadam">macadam</a>. Later, <a href="/wiki/Tarmacadam" title="Tarmacadam">tarmac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a> became the dominant paving materials. 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:First_flight2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4280" data-file-width="6603" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/First_flight2.jpg/220px-First_flight2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/First_flight2.jpg/330px-First_flight2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/First_flight2.jpg/440px-First_flight2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:First_flight2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wright brothers</a>' first flight in 1903</div></div></div>
<p>In 1903 the <a href="/wiki/Wright_brothers" title="Wright brothers">Wright brothers</a> demonstrated the first successful controllable <a href="/wiki/Airplane" title="Airplane">airplane</a>, and after World War I (1914–1918) aircraft became a fast way to transport people and express goods over long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>After World War II (1939–1945) the automobile and airlines took higher shares of transport, reducing rail and water to freight and short-haul passenger services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooper277_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-cooper277-47">[47]</a></sup> Scientific spaceflight began in the 1950s, with rapid growth until the 1970s, when interest dwindled. In the 1950s the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Containerization" title="Containerization">containerization</a> gave massive efficiency gains in freight transport, fostering <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">globalization</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bcn21114_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-bcn21114-31">[31]</a></sup> International air travel became much more accessible in the 1960s with the commercialization of the <a href="/wiki/Jet_engine" title="Jet engine">jet engine</a>. Along with the growth in automobiles and motorways, rail and water transport declined in relative importance. After the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Shinkansen" title="Shinkansen">Shinkansen</a> in Japan in 1964, high-speed rail in Asia and Europe started attracting passengers on long-haul routes away from the airlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cooper277_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-cooper277-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._history" title="U.S. history">U.S. history</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Privately_held_company" title="Privately held company">private</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joint-stock" title="Joint-stock">joint-stock</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corporations" title="Corporations">corporations</a> owned most <a href="/wiki/Navigable_aqueduct" title="Navigable aqueduct">aqueducts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bridge" title="Bridge">bridges</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canals" title="Canals">canals</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Railroads" title="Railroads">railroads</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roads" title="Roads">roads</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunnels" title="Tunnels">tunnels</a>. Most such transport infrastructure came under government control in the late 19th and early 20th centuries, culminating in the <a href="/wiki/Nationalization" title="Nationalization">nationalization</a> of inter-city passenger rail-service with the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a>. Recently,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (February 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> however, a movement to privatize roads and other infrastructure has gained some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="This term requires quantification. (February 2016)">quantify</span></a></i>]</sup> ground and adherents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
